Intelligent Conversational AI for KSP Crime Database
The Challenge:

Design and develop an Intelligent Conversational AI and Crime Analytics Platform that enables investigators, analysts, and policymakers to interact with the state crime database using natural language queries, while also providing advanced analytical capabilities grounded in criminology and sociological insights. 

The proposed solution should enable users to discover hidden relationships between crimes,  offenders, victims, locations, and socio-economic patterns, support investigative decision making, and provide predictive and preventive insights to strengthen proactive law enforcement. 

The platform should go beyond simple data retrieval and enable: 

• Crime paDern discovery 

• Criminal network analysis 

• Socio-demographic crime insights 

• Behavioral and criminological profiling 

• Proactive crime prevention intelligence 



Proposed Expected Solution Framework 

1. Conversational Crime Intelligence Interface 

• Natural language chatbot interface for querying crime records in English and regional languages. 

• Ability to retrieve information about FIRs, accused, victims, locations, investigation status, and criminal history.

• Context-aware conversations allowing follow-up queries without repeating context. • Save the Conversation History in PDF format locally 

• Support for Multiple Languages, English and Kannada to start with • Support Voice interaction for the Q&A 

2. Criminal Network & Relationship Analysis 

• Identification of links between accused, victims, locations, financial accounts, and crime incidents. 

• Visualization of criminal networks and associations. 

• Detection of organized crime groups and repeat offender networks. 3. Crime PaDern & Trend Analy-cs 

• Analysis of crime trends across -me, geography, crime type, and modus operandi. • Identification of crime hotspots and emerging crime clusters. 

• Seasonal and event-based crime trend analysis. 

4. Sociological Crime Insights 

• Analysis of crime patterns based on demographic attributes such as age, gender, and socio-economic background. 

• Identification of social risk factors influencing crime patterns. 

• Correlation of crime with urbanization, migration, economic stress, education and other social indicators. 

5. Criminology-Based Offender Profiling 

• Identification of repeat offenders and habitual criminals. 

• Behavioral analysis of offenders based on crime history and modus operandi. • Risk scoring of offenders to prioritize investigation. 

6. Inves-gator Decision Support 

• Automated case summaries and inves-ga-on -melines. 

• Identification of similar past cases and investigation outcomes. 

• Recommenda-ons for poten-al inves-ga-ve leads. 

7. Financial Crime & Transaction Link Analysis 

• Detection of financial transactions linked to criminal activities.

• Identification of money trails and suspicious transaction networks. 

• Integrate with financial crime investigation workflows. 

8. Crime Forecasting & Early Warning 

• AI-driven identification of emerging crime patterns. 

• Early warning alerts for repeat crimes, gang activity, or organized crime. • Predic-ve analysis of poten-al crime hotspots. 

9. Explainable AI & Transparent Analytics 

• All AI responses are supported by clear data references and evidence trails. • Visualization of reasoning paths and correlations used in analysis. 

• Compliance with law enforcement accountability requirements. 

10. Secure Role-Based Access & Governance 

• Role-based access for investigators, analysts, supervisors, and policymakers. • Secure handling of sensitive data with audit logs and traceability.

## Response:
06/07/2026, 09:39:27

Imagine you have a giant 📚 magic notebook that contains **every crime that happened in Karnataka**.

Inside the notebook are things like:
- 🚔 Police complaints (FIRs)
- 👤 Who did the crime
- 🙋 Who was affected
- 📍 Where it happened
- 📅 When it happened
- 💰 Money involved
- 🚗 Vehicles
- 📞 Phone numbers
- 🏠 Addresses
- 📝 Investigation updates

The problem is...

There are **millions of pages**. 😵

If a police officer wants to find something, they may spend hours searching.

So this challenge asks us to build a **super-smart AI detective assistant** 🤖🕵️.

---

# Imagine the AI is like Batman's computer 🦇

Instead of typing difficult computer commands, a police officer can simply ask:

> "Show me all bike thefts in Bengaluru during the last month."

The AI instantly finds the answer.

Or ask:

> "Has this person committed crimes before?"

The AI checks everything.

Or ask:

> "Are these two cases connected?"

The AI investigates and explains.

That's the main idea.

---

# Let's understand every feature

## 1. Smart Chatbot 💬

Imagine talking to Google, but only about crime data.

Police can ask:

> "Who was arrested in Case 2025-145?"

AI answers.

Then they ask:

> "Where does he live?"

The AI remembers they are talking about the same person.

No need to repeat.

It should also:

🎤 Understand voice

🗣 Understand English and Kannada

📄 Save the whole conversation as a PDF

---

## 2. Criminal Network Analysis 🕸

Imagine drawing a spider web.

One criminal knows another.

They share the same phone number.

They use the same bank account.

They use the same vehicle.

They commit crimes in the same area.

The AI draws all these connections.

Example:

```
Person A
   |
Phone Number
   |
Person B
   |
Bank Account
   |
Person C
```

Now police discover that three criminals are working together.

---

## 3. Crime Pattern Analysis 📈

Suppose there are:

- 2 robberies in January
- 8 robberies in February
- 20 robberies in March

The AI notices:

> "Robberies are increasing."

Or:

Every Saturday there are many thefts.

Or:

One market has many phone snatching cases.

Humans may miss this.

AI finds it automatically.

---

## 4. Sociology Insights 👨👩👧👦

Now the AI asks:

Why is crime happening?

Maybe:

- unemployment
- poverty
- school dropout
- migration
- crowded cities

The AI studies these patterns.

Example:

A district with high unemployment has more theft cases.

The AI reports that relationship.

---

## 5. Criminal Profiling 🧠

Suppose someone has:

- stolen bikes 8 times
- always works at night
- always targets the same area

The AI says:

"This criminal usually steals motorcycles after 11 PM near railway stations."

Now police know what to expect.

It can also give a **risk score**.

Example:

```
Risk Score

Rahul : 95/100 🔴

Ajay : 40/100 🟡

Ramesh : 10/100 🟢
```

High score means police should pay more attention.

---

## 6. Investigator Assistant 🕵️

Police officers have lots of paperwork.

Instead of reading 500 pages...

AI makes a summary.

Example:

**Case Summary**

- FIR filed on June 2
- Victim reported theft
- CCTV collected
- Suspect identified
- Charge sheet pending

Done in seconds.

The AI can also say:

"This looks similar to a burglary case from last year."

---

## 7. Financial Crime Analysis 💸

Imagine money moving between many bank accounts.

```
Account A
   ↓
Account B
   ↓
Account C
   ↓
Criminal
```

Humans may not notice.

The AI detects suspicious money trails.

Useful for:

- cyber fraud
- money laundering
- scam investigations

---

## 8. Crime Prediction 🔮

This is like weather forecasting, but for crime.

Weather says:

"It may rain tomorrow."

AI says:

"This area may experience more bike thefts next week because similar patterns happened before."

Police can patrol that area early.

This helps prevent crime.

---

## 9. Explainable AI 📖

Very important.

The AI cannot simply say:

> "I think this person is suspicious."

It must explain why.

Example:

```
Reason:

• Same phone number as accused
• Same vehicle
• Present at crime location
• Previous FIR in similar case
```

Everything must be supported by evidence.

---

## 10. Security 🔐

Not everyone should see all crime data.

Different users have different permissions.

👮 Investigator

Can see full case details.

👨‍💼 Supervisor

Can see all district reports.

📊 Analyst

Can study patterns but may not access sensitive personal details.

Every action is recorded.

If someone misuses the system, administrators know exactly who accessed what.

---

# In one simple story 📚

Imagine a police officer named Ravi.

Instead of searching thousands of files, Ravi asks:

> "Show me all phone snatching cases near Majestic Bus Stand."

The AI replies instantly.

Ravi then asks:

> "Any repeat offenders?"

AI finds three.

Then:

> "Are they connected?"

AI draws a network.

Then:

> "Any similar crimes last year?"

AI shows five matching cases.

Then:

> "Which area is likely to have the next incident?"

AI predicts a nearby hotspot.

Finally:

> "Summarize everything."

AI creates a report and saves it as a PDF.

What used to take hours can now take minutes.

---

### 🌟 The whole challenge in one sentence

Build an **AI-powered detective assistant** that lets police talk naturally with crime data, uncovers hidden links between people and crimes, analyzes trends, predicts future risks, explains its reasoning with evidence, and helps investigators solve cases faster and make better decisions.

## 🚀 How we'll continue

I recommend we treat this as a complete software engineering journey rather than jumping between features.

We'll build it in small milestones:

1. **Phase 0:** Foundation (current)
2. **Phase 1:** Database setup and importing the KSP dataset
3. **Phase 2:** Build the FastAPI backend and REST APIs
4. **Phase 3:** Build the React dashboard
5. **Phase 4:** Connect the LLM for conversational queries (NL → SQL)
6. **Phase 5:** Add voice support and Kannada
7. **Phase 6:** Build criminal network analysis with Neo4j
8. **Phase 7:** Add crime analytics, forecasting, and investigator tools
9. **Phase 8:** Deploy everything on Zoho Catalyst and prepare the hackathon demo

We'll make each phase hands-on, with complete code, explanations, folder structure, database schema, API design, and testing so that by the end you'll have a production-style project rather than just a prototype.

# Day 1 - Lesson 1

Forget the database.

Forget AI.

Let's imagine a real police station.

---

A person walks into the police station.

👨: "Sir, my bike was stolen."

What does the police officer do first?

He creates an **FIR**.

That FIR becomes **one case**.

In your database, **one FIR = one CaseMaster record**. `Police_FIR_ER_Diagram.pdf`

So think like this:

```
Real Life

Bike Stolen
      ↓
Police writes FIR
      ↓
One Case
```

Database:

```
Bike Stolen
      ↓
CaseMaster
```

**CaseMaster is the heart of the entire system.** Every other table connects to it. `Police_FIR_ER_Diagram.pdf`

---

# Think of CaseMaster as a Folder 📁

Imagine this folder:

```
Case 101

Inside

✓ Crime Number
✓ Date
✓ Police Station
✓ Court
✓ Crime Type
✓ Status
✓ Location
✓ Brief Facts
```

That's exactly what **CaseMaster** stores. It contains the basic information about a crime, such as the crime number, registration date, police station, crime category, status, court, GPS location, and a brief summary. `Police_FIR_ER_Diagram.pdf` `Police_FIR_ER_Diagram.pdf`

---

# Now imagine the police asks:

"Who complained?"

That information is **NOT** stored inside CaseMaster.

Instead, it goes into another table.

```
ComplainantDetails
```

This table stores:

- Name
- Age
- Occupation
- Religion
- Gender

and links back to the same case using **CaseMasterID**. `Police_FIR_ER_Diagram.pdf`

Think:

```
Case 101

↓

Complaint by

Rahul
Age 32
Farmer
```

---

# Then police asks:

"Who is the victim?"

Another table.

```
Victim
```

It stores:

```
Victim Name

Age

Gender
```

Each victim also belongs to the same **CaseMasterID**. `Police_FIR_ER_Diagram.pdf`

---

# Then police catches someone.

Where is that stored?

```
Accused
```

It stores:

```
Accused Name

Age

Gender
```

and links that accused person to the case. `Police_FIR_ER_Diagram.pdf`

---

# Now look at everything together.

Imagine this one robbery case.

```
CaseMaster
│
├── Crime No : 104430...
├── Police Station : Whitefield
├── Crime : Robbery
│
├── Complainant
│      Ravi
│
├── Victim
│      Anil
│
└── Accused
       Ramesh
```

This is how the police database thinks.

## Response:
06/07/2026, 11:54:18

🎉 Exactly! That's the correct way to think.

Let's see why.

---

## Story Time 📖

Ravi buys a new phone.

One day someone steals it.

Ravi goes to the police station.

The police officer asks:

👮 "Who is reporting this crime?"

Answer:

👉 Ravi

So Ravi is stored in:

```text
ComplainantDetails
```

because **he filed the complaint**. `Police_FIR_ER_Diagram.pdf`

---

Then the officer asks:

👮 "Who suffered because of this crime?"

Answer:

👉 Ravi

So Ravi is also stored in:

```text
Victim
```

because **he is the victim**. `Police_FIR_ER_Diagram.pdf`

---

So the database looks like this:

```text
CaseMaster (Case #101)
        │
        ├──────────────┐
        │              │
ComplainantDetails   Victim
       Ravi           Ravi
```

Same person.

Different **roles**.

---

# Here's another example.

Imagine a jewelry shop.

An employee named **Arun** works there.

One night, thieves rob the shop.

The shop owner goes to the police station.

Who filed the complaint?

👉 Shop Owner

Who is the victim?

👉 Arun (employee who was attacked)

or maybe

👉 The jewelry shop/company

Now the database becomes:

```text
CaseMaster

↓

Complainant
Shop Owner

↓

Victim
Arun
```

Notice that the complainant and victim can be **different people**.

---

# 💡 This is an important lesson

A database doesn't ask:

> "Who is this person?"

It asks:

> "What role does this person play in this case?"

The same person can have multiple roles in the same case.

---

# Now let's learn the most important thing in the whole database.

Look at this:

```text
CaseMasterID = 1001
```

Suppose the police created one case.

Everything related to that case gets the same **CaseMasterID**.

Example:

```text
CaseMaster
-------------
CaseMasterID = 1001
Crime = Robbery

ComplainantDetails
------------------
CaseMasterID = 1001
Name = Ravi

Victim
------------------
CaseMasterID = 1001
Name = Ravi

Accused
------------------
CaseMasterID = 1001
Name = Suresh
```

Can you see the pattern?

**CaseMasterID is like the glue 🩹 that sticks everything together.**

Without it, the database wouldn't know which victim, complainant, or accused belongs to which case. The ER diagram shows that `Victim`, `Accused`, `ComplainantDetails`, `ArrestSurrender`, and several other tables all reference `CaseMasterID` to link back to the same FIR. `Police_FIR_ER_Diagram.pdf`

---

## 🧠 Tiny Quiz (don't overthink it)

Imagine this case:

- Crime No: 104430006202600001
- Complainant: Rahul
- Victims: Rahul and Priya
- Accused: Arjun, Kiran, and Mohan

**Question:**

How many records do you think will be created in each table?

Fill this in:

```text
CaseMaster = ?

ComplainantDetails = ?

Victim = ?

Accused = ?
```

Try answering. This one question will make you understand **80% of how the database works**.

## Prompt:
06/07/2026, 11:55:27

CaseMaster = 1 

ComplainantDetails = 1 

Victim = 2 

Accused = 3

## Response:
06/07/2026, 11:55:29

🥳 YES!! 100% Correct!!

You just understood one of the hardest concepts in relational databases.

Let's see why.

| Table | Number of Records | Why |
|-------|-------------------:|-----|
| CaseMaster | ✅ 1 | One FIR = One Case |
| ComplainantDetails | ✅ 1 | Rahul filed the complaint |
| Victim | ✅ 2 | Rahul and Priya are victims |
| Accused | ✅ 3 | Three accused persons | `Police_FIR_ER_Diagram.pdf`

So the database looks like this:

```text
                 CaseMaster
              (CaseMasterID = 1001)
                      │
        ┌─────────────┼──────────────┐
        │             │              │
 Complainant      Victims        Accused
     Rahul      Rahul, Priya   Arjun, Kiran, Mohan
```

Notice something amazing?

Everything belongs to **one CaseMasterID**.

That is why **CaseMaster is called the parent table**.

---

# 🎉 Congratulations!

You have now understood:

- ✅ What an FIR is
- ✅ What CaseMaster is
- ✅ What a parent table is
- ✅ What child tables are
- ✅ Why CaseMasterID is important
- ✅ One-to-Many relationships

These are the basics that many beginners struggle with.

---

# Phase 0 - Part 2

Now ask yourself one question:

> **"How will my AI answer a police officer's question?"**

Suppose an investigator asks:

> **"Show me all murder cases in Bengaluru registered in January 2026."**

Does the AI magically know the answer?

❌ No.

It follows a pipeline.

```text
Police Officer
      │
      ▼
Types a question
      │
      ▼
AI understands the question
      │
      ▼
Converts it into a database query
      │
      ▼
Searches the database
      │
      ▼
Gets matching records
      │
      ▼
Explains the result in simple language
```

This is the **heart of your project**.

---

# Now let's identify the building blocks.

Imagine we're building a car. 🚗

What do we need?

### 1. Database 🗄️

Stores all crime records.

Example:
- Cases
- Victims
- Accused
- Courts

---

### 2. Backend ⚙️

Acts like the manager.

When someone asks a question, it:
- receives the request,
- talks to the database,
- sends the answer back.

---

### 3. AI Brain 🤖

It translates:

> "Show robbery cases"

into something the database understands.

---

### 4. Frontend 🖥️

The screen the police officer sees.

It has:
- Chat window
- Maps
- Crime charts
- Network graph

---

# Together it looks like this

```text
Police Officer
        │
        ▼
Frontend (Website)
        │
        ▼
Backend (FastAPI)
        │
 ┌──────┴──────┐
 ▼             ▼
AI         PostgreSQL
 │             │
 └──────┬──────┘
        ▼
 Answer
```

# 🚨 Before writing any code

Imagine the police officer asks:

> **"How many theft cases happened in Mysuru?"**

Who should calculate the answer?

### A.

The AI (LLM) should remember all crime records and answer directly.

### B.

The database should calculate the answer, and the AI should only explain the result.

Which one do you think is correct, and **why**?

## Prompt:
06/07/2026, 11:57:15

The database should calculate the answer, and the AI should only explain the result.   because ai has less context memory

## Response:
06/07/2026, 11:57:17

🔥 Excellent! You're thinking in the right direction.

Your answer is **correct**, but not only because of context memory.

The **real reason** is even more important.

---

## Imagine this

The database has **10 crore (100 million) crime records**.

Now a police officer asks:

> "How many theft cases happened in Mysuru?"

If the AI tries to remember all those records...

🤯 Impossible.

Even the biggest LLMs cannot keep an entire police database in memory.

---

## So who is the expert?

Think of it like a classroom.

👨‍🏫 Teacher (AI)

The teacher doesn't memorize every student's marks.

Instead, the teacher asks the school office.

🏢 School Office (Database)

The office has the official records.

The office says:

> "There are 432 theft cases."

Then the teacher tells the student:

> "There were **432 theft cases in Mysuru**."

Exactly the same thing happens here.

```text
Police Officer
      │
      ▼
AI
      │
      ▼
Database
      │
      ▼
Returns 432
      │
      ▼
AI says:
"There were 432 theft cases."
```

---

## The AI's real job

The AI is **not a database**.

The AI is a **translator**.

For example:

Police asks:

> "Show me murder cases in Bengaluru."

AI translates that into something like:

```sql
SELECT *
FROM CaseMaster
WHERE CrimeType = 'Murder'
AND District = 'Bengaluru';
```

The **database** executes the query.

The AI then explains the result in normal English (or Kannada).

---

# This is the architecture you'll build

```text
            Police Officer
                  │
                  ▼
        "Show murder cases"
                  │
                  ▼
        🤖 AI understands English
                  │
                  ▼
      Converts to SQL Query
                  │
                  ▼
      🗄️ PostgreSQL Database
                  │
                  ▼
      Returns matching records
                  │
                  ▼
     🤖 AI explains the answer
                  │
                  ▼
           Police Officer
```

---

# 💡 Here's the biggest misconception beginners have

Many people think:

> "I'll upload my database to the AI."

❌ That's not how production systems work.

Instead:

- The **database stores the data**.
- The **AI understands human language**.
- The **backend connects the AI and the database**.

This design is:
- ✅ Faster
- ✅ More accurate
- ✅ More secure
- ✅ Scales to millions of records

---

Yes, **you can build this project on your PC**, but you'll need to be smart about what runs locally and what runs in the cloud.

### Your PC
- 💻 **CPU:** Intel Core i5 (3rd Gen) — ✅ Good enough for development.
- 🧠 **RAM:** 8 GB — ✅ Usable, but you'll need to avoid running too many heavy programs at once.
- 🎮 **GPU:** NVIDIA GT 610 (2 GB) — ❌ Too old for AI model training or running large LLMs locally.

## What will run fine? ✅

| Task | Can your PC handle it? |
|------|-------------------------|
| VS Code | ✅ Yes |
| Python | ✅ Yes |
| PostgreSQL | ✅ Yes |
| FastAPI | ✅ Yes |
| React frontend | ✅ Yes |
| Git | ✅ Yes |
| Neo4j | ✅ Yes (small datasets) |
| Zoho Catalyst development | ✅ Yes |

## What will NOT run well? ❌

| Task | Status |
|------|--------|
| Running large LLMs like Llama 3 8B locally | ❌ No |
| Fine-tuning AI models | ❌ No |
| Training deep learning models | ❌ No |

Your PC does all the application work, and the AI model runs on cloud servers.

Awesome! 🔥 You're making real progress.

But before we create `001_casemaster.sql`, I want to make one important change to our approach.

## ❌ I don't want to blindly copy the ER diagram into PostgreSQL.

Why?

Because the ER diagram is designed for the **actual Karnataka Police production system**, which likely has **60–100+ tables**. If we recreate every table now, we'll spend weeks building the database and **still won't have a working application**.

Instead, let's think like a startup building an **MVP (Minimum Viable Product)**.

---

# 🏆 Our Hackathon Goal

We don't need all 100 tables.

We need enough tables to demonstrate:

- ✅ Conversational AI
- ✅ FIR search
- ✅ Criminal history
- ✅ Network analysis
- ✅ Crime analytics
- ✅ Case summary

---

# So we'll build in layers.

## 🟢 Layer 1 (Core Investigation)

```text
CaseMaster
Person
CasePerson
Act
Section
CaseSection
Arrest
```

Notice something?

I replaced:

- Victim
- Complainant
- Accused

with one table:

```text
Person
```

and another table:

```text
CasePerson
```

---

## Why?

Let's think.

Imagine Ravi.

Case 1:
- Ravi is a Victim.

Case 2:
- Ravi is a Complainant.

Case 3:
- Ravi becomes an Accused.

Should we store Ravi three times?

❌ No.

Instead:

```text
Person
---------
PersonID
Name
DOB
Gender
Address
```

Then:

```text
CasePerson
-------------
CaseID
PersonID
Role

Role =
Victim
Accused
Witness
Complainant
```

Now Ravi exists **once**.

His role changes depending on the case.

This is a more normalized design.

---

# Why is this better?

Imagine later the police ask:

> "Show every case involving Ravi."

With separate tables, you'd have to search:

- Victim
- Accused
- Complainant
- Witness

With one `Person` table:

```sql
SELECT *
FROM CasePerson
WHERE PersonID = 101;
```

Done.

---

# This is how many modern systems are designed.

The original ER diagram separates roles because it reflects operational workflows.

For **our AI platform**, a normalized design is simpler and more powerful.

---

# 🏗️ Our Database (Version 1)

```text
CaseMaster
     │
     │
CasePerson
     │
     │
   Person
```

Then:

```text
CaseMaster
      │
CaseSection
      │
   Section
      │
     Act
```

And:

```text
CaseMaster
      │
   Arrest
```

---

# What this gives us

We can already answer:

✅ Who are all the accused?

✅ Who is the victim?

✅ Show Ravi's criminal history.

✅ Show everyone connected to Case X.

✅ Show all FIRs under Section 379.

✅ Build criminal networks.

---

# 🚨 This is a key architectural decision

If this were a production system replacing KSP's existing database, we'd mirror their schema.

But **we're not replacing their system**.

We're building an **AI layer** on top of crime data.

For a hackathon, a clean, normalized schema is easier to build, query, and explain.

---

## 🎯 Your next task (before we write SQL)

I want you to answer one question:

Suppose **Rahul appears in 20 different FIRs**.

Which design do you think is better?

### Option A
Store Rahul's name, address, and age separately in every Victim/Accused/Complainant table.

### Option B
Store Rahul **once** in a `Person` table, and connect him to cases through a `CasePerson` table with a role.

Which would you choose, and why?

This is exactly the kind of database design question a software architect asks before writing the first table.

## Prompt:
06/07/2026, 19:00:13

B because multiple times name repates the system crashes

## Response:
06/07/2026, 19:00:13

👏 Good thinking! You chose the **correct option (B)**.

The reason is slightly different, though.

> **The system won't crash because a name repeats.**

Databases are designed to store millions of repeated values. Repeated names don't cause crashes.

The real problems are:

### Option A ❌

Imagine Rahul appears in 20 FIRs.

```text
Victim Table
Rahul
Rahul
Rahul

Accused Table
Rahul
Rahul

Complainant Table
Rahul
Rahul
```

Now Rahul changes his address.

You have to update it in **every row**.

If you miss one row, the database becomes inconsistent.

---

### Option B ✅

```text
Person
----------------
PersonID: 101
Name: Rahul
Address: Whitefield

CasePerson
----------------
Case1 -> Rahul -> Victim
Case2 -> Rahul -> Complainant
Case3 -> Rahul -> Accused
Case4 -> Rahul -> Witness
```

Now Rahul's information exists **only once**.

If his address changes:

✅ Update one row.

Done.

---

# This is called **Database Normalization**.

In simple words:

> **Store information once, then create relationships instead of making duplicate copies.**

This makes the database:
- ✅ Faster to maintain
- ✅ Smaller
- ✅ More accurate
- ✅ Easier to query

---

## 🚨 Now, one important thing

I'm going to slightly change our direction.

The **ER diagram you have is the official KSP schema**. For the hackathon, judges may appreciate that your solution aligns with it.

So here's what we'll do:

- **Internally (our AI layer):** We can use a normalized model like `Person` + `CasePerson` if it simplifies analytics.
- **When importing KSP data:** We'll preserve the original structure from the ER diagram so it matches the official database.

This gives us the best of both worlds:
- Compatible with KSP's data.
- Easier AI and analytics.

```sql
CREATE DATABASE ksp_crime_ai;
```

# Step 2: Create `001_casemaster.sql`

Create the file:

```text
database/schema/001_casemaster.sql
```

Paste this:

```sql
CREATE TABLE CaseMaster (
    CaseMasterID SERIAL PRIMARY KEY,
    FIRNumber VARCHAR(50) UNIQUE NOT NULL,
    CrimeType VARCHAR(100) NOT NULL,
    CrimeDate DATE NOT NULL,
    PoliceStation VARCHAR(150),
    District VARCHAR(100),
    State VARCHAR(100) DEFAULT 'Karnataka',
    Latitude DECIMAL(10,7),
    Longitude DECIMAL(10,7),
    InvestigationStatus VARCHAR(50),
    BriefFacts TEXT,
    CreatedAt TIMESTAMP DEFAULT CURRENT_TIMESTAMP
);
```

Then execute it.

---

# Step 3: Verify

Run:

```sql
SELECT * FROM CaseMaster;
```

It should return:

```text
0 rows
```

That's **good**. The table exists but has no data yet.

---

# Step 4: Insert Your First FIR

Run:

```sql
INSERT INTO CaseMaster (
    FIRNumber,
    CrimeType,
    CrimeDate,
    PoliceStation,
    District,
    InvestigationStatus,
    BriefFacts
)
VALUES (
    'FIR202600001',
    'Theft',
    '2026-07-07',
    'Whitefield Police Station',
    'Bengaluru Urban',
    'Open',
    'Motorcycle stolen from parking area.'
);
```

Now verify:

```sql
SELECT * FROM CaseMaster;
```

You should see one row.

# 📂 Database Structure

Create these files:

```text
database/
└── schema/
    ├── 001_state.sql
    ├── 002_district.sql
    ├── 003_police_station.sql
    ├── 004_case_master.sql
    ├── 005_person.sql
    ├── 006_case_person.sql
    ├── 007_act.sql
    ├── 008_section.sql
    ├── 009_case_section.sql
    └── 010_arrest.sql
```

---

# Why this order?

Imagine building a family tree.

You can't create a child before creating the parent.

Example:

```text
State
   │
District
   │
Police Station
   │
CaseMaster
```

`CaseMaster` needs a police station.

A police station belongs to a district.

A district belongs to a state.

So we create them in that order.

# 🚀 Sprint 1 - Day 1

## Milestone:
**Create the first real database schema.**

We'll create **4 tables**:

```text
State
   │
District
   │
PoliceStation
   │
CaseMaster
```

These are the foundation for everything else.

---

# Step 1: Create `001_state.sql`

```sql
CREATE TABLE State (
    state_id SERIAL PRIMARY KEY,
    state_name VARCHAR(100) NOT NULL UNIQUE
);
```

Run it.

---

# Step 2: Create `002_district.sql`

```sql
CREATE TABLE District (
    district_id SERIAL PRIMARY KEY,
    district_name VARCHAR(100) NOT NULL,
    state_id INT NOT NULL,
    FOREIGN KEY (state_id)
        REFERENCES State(state_id)
        ON DELETE CASCADE
);
```

Run it.

---

# Step 3: Create `003_police_station.sql`

```sql
CREATE TABLE PoliceStation (
    station_id SERIAL PRIMARY KEY,
    station_name VARCHAR(150) NOT NULL,
    district_id INT NOT NULL,
    address TEXT,

    FOREIGN KEY (district_id)
        REFERENCES District(district_id)
        ON DELETE CASCADE
);
```

Run it.

---

# Step 4: Create `004_case_master.sql`

```sql
CREATE TABLE CaseMaster (
    case_id SERIAL PRIMARY KEY,

    fir_number VARCHAR(50) UNIQUE NOT NULL,

    crime_type VARCHAR(100) NOT NULL,

    crime_date DATE NOT NULL,

    station_id INT NOT NULL,

    investigation_status VARCHAR(50),

    brief_facts TEXT,

    created_at TIMESTAMP DEFAULT CURRENT_TIMESTAMP,

    FOREIGN KEY (station_id)
        REFERENCES PoliceStation(station_id)
);
```

Run it.

---

# Step 5: Check the tables

In PostgreSQL, execute:

```sql
SELECT table_name
FROM information_schema.tables
WHERE table_schema='public';
```

You should see:

```text
State
District
PoliceStation
CaseMaster
```

---

# 🎓 What you learned today

You just learned one of the most important concepts in databases:

```
State
   │
District
   │
PoliceStation
   │
CaseMaster
```

This is called **referential integrity**.

It means:

- A district **must** belong to a state.
- A police station **must** belong to a district.
- A case **must** belong to a police station.

This prevents invalid data.

---

Since your project is based on the KSP ER diagram, I want our schema to stay as close as possible to it.

**Can you upload the ER Diagram PDF here?**

I know you mentioned you only have the ER diagram. If you upload it, we'll:
1. Recreate the schema accurately.
2. Avoid inventing fields that don't exist.
3. Build a database that matches the official design while still being suitable for AI and analytics.

This will save us from redesigning tables later and make your hackathon project much stronger.

These tables don't depend much on case data and everything else references them.

# 📂 Create these SQL files

```text
database/schema/
│
├── 001_lookup_tables.sql
├── 002_unit_tables.sql
├── 003_employee.sql
├── 004_case_master.sql
├── 005_people.sql
├── 006_legal.sql
├── 007_arrest.sql
├── 008_seed_data.sql
```

---

# 📋 File 1: `001_lookup_tables.sql`

This file creates all the master/lookup tables.

```sql
CREATE TABLE State (
    StateID SERIAL PRIMARY KEY,
    StateName VARCHAR(100) NOT NULL,
    NationalityID INT,
    Active BOOLEAN DEFAULT TRUE
);

CREATE TABLE District (
    DistrictID SERIAL PRIMARY KEY,
    DistrictName VARCHAR(100) NOT NULL,
    StateID INT NOT NULL REFERENCES State(StateID),
    Active BOOLEAN DEFAULT TRUE
);

CREATE TABLE CaseStatusMaster (
    CaseStatusID SERIAL PRIMARY KEY,
    CaseStatusName VARCHAR(100) NOT NULL
);

CREATE TABLE CaseCategory (
    CaseCategoryID SERIAL PRIMARY KEY,
    LookupValue VARCHAR(100) NOT NULL
);

CREATE TABLE GravityOffence (
    GravityOffenceID SERIAL PRIMARY KEY,
    LookupValue VARCHAR(100) NOT NULL
);

CREATE TABLE CrimeHead (
    CrimeHeadID SERIAL PRIMARY KEY,
    CrimeGroupName VARCHAR(150),
    Active BOOLEAN DEFAULT TRUE
);

CREATE TABLE CrimeSubHead (
    CrimeSubHeadID SERIAL PRIMARY KEY,
    CrimeHeadID INT REFERENCES CrimeHead(CrimeHeadID),
    CrimeHeadName VARCHAR(150),
    SeqID INT
);
```

Run it.

If there are **no errors**, stop there.

---

# 📌 Why are we doing only one file today?

Because I want every milestone to end with a **working database**.

Many hackathon teams write 5,000 lines of SQL, hit one error, and spend hours debugging.

Instead we'll do:

```
One file
↓

Execute

↓

Verify

↓

Commit to Git
```

That's how professional teams work.

You should now see the lookup tables listed.

# 🛑 One important change

I want to **stop writing SQL manually**.

Instead, we'll use **SQLAlchemy models** in Python.

Why?

Because SQLAlchemy can create the PostgreSQL tables automatically, and it's easier to maintain as the project grows.

The workflow becomes:

```text
Python Model
      │
      ▼
SQLAlchemy
      │
      ▼
PostgreSQL Tables
```

This is how many professional FastAPI projects are built.

# 🚨 One important suggestion

From now on, **don't create tables directly in pgAdmin**.

Since you've chosen **SQLAlchemy**, let SQLAlchemy become the **single source of truth**.

The professional workflow is:

```text
Python Model
      ↓
Alembic Migration
      ↓
PostgreSQL Database
```

This has big advantages:
- ✅ Your database structure is version-controlled in Git.
- ✅ Team members (or future you) can recreate the database easily.
- ✅ Schema changes are tracked and reversible.
- ✅ It's the standard approach for FastAPI projects.

Since you've already created a few tables manually, that's okay for learning. But before we go much further, we'll move those definitions into SQLAlchemy models and use Alembic migrations going forward.

## 📌 Here's the plan for the next milestone

We'll **stop creating more tables manually** and instead:

1. Configure SQLAlchemy.
2. Connect it to PostgreSQL.
3. Create the first model (`CaseMaster`).
4. Generate a migration with Alembic.
5. Let Alembic create/update the database.

That sets up a professional development workflow for the rest of the project. 🚀

# Step 1: Create `base.py`

Inside:

```text
backend/app/database/base.py
```

```python
from sqlalchemy.orm import DeclarativeBase

class Base(DeclarativeBase):
    pass
```

This is the parent class for all SQLAlchemy models.

---

# Step 2: Update `connection.py`

Add this at the bottom of your `connection.py`:

```python
from app.database.base import Base

def create_tables():
    Base.metadata.create_all(bind=engine)
```

Now SQLAlchemy knows how to create every table.

---

# Step 3: Update `main.py`

Your `main.py` is already good. `main.py`

Just add startup code:

```python
from app.database.connection import create_tables

app = FastAPI(
    title="KSP Crime Intelligence Platform",
    version="1.0.0"
)

@app.on_event("startup")
def startup():
    create_tables()
```

When FastAPI starts, it will automatically create tables if they don't exist.

---

# Step 4: Improve the Case model

Instead of:

```python
id
crime_number
crime_type
district
```

Let's make it closer to the KSP schema:

```python
from sqlalchemy import Column, Integer, String, Date
from app.database.base import Base

class Case(Base):

    __tablename__ = "casemaster"

    case_id = Column(Integer, primary_key=True, index=True)

    fir_number = Column(String(50), unique=True, nullable=False)

    crime_type = Column(String(100))

    district = Column(String(100))

    police_station = Column(String(100))

    investigation_status = Column(String(50))

    crime_date = Column(Date)
```

Later we'll expand this model with all the ER diagram fields.

---

# Step 5: Create your first Repository

Create:

```text
backend/app/repositories/case_repository.py
```

```python
from sqlalchemy.orm import Session
from app.models.case import Case

def get_all_cases(db: Session):
    return db.query(Case).all()
```

---

# Step 6: Update the API

In your `cases.py` router:

```python
from fastapi import APIRouter, Depends
from sqlalchemy.orm import Session

from app.database.connection import get_db
from app.repositories.case_repository import get_all_cases

router = APIRouter(prefix="/cases", tags=["Cases"])

@router.get("/")
def list_cases(db: Session = Depends(get_db)):
    return get_all_cases(db)
```

---

# Step 7: Test

Start the server:

```bash
uvicorn app.main:app --reload
```

Open:

```
http://127.0.0.1:8000/docs
```

You should see:

```
GET /cases
```

```text
Service Layer
```

# Step 2: It should look like this

```python
from sqlalchemy import create_engine
from sqlalchemy.orm import sessionmaker
from dotenv import load_dotenv
from pathlib import Path
import os

from app.database.base import Base

BASE_DIR = Path(__file__).resolve().parent.parent.parent

load_dotenv(BASE_DIR / ".env")

DATABASE_URL = os.getenv("DATABASE_URL")

engine = create_engine(DATABASE_URL)

SessionLocal = sessionmaker(
    autocommit=False,
    autoflush=False,
    bind=engine
)

def get_db():
    db = SessionLocal()
    try:
        yield db
    finally:
        db.close()

def create_tables():
    Base.metadata.create_all(bind=engine)
```

Notice it contains **both**:

- ✅ `get_db()`
- ✅ `create_tables()`

## Response:
07/07/2026, 12:56:09

🎯 We found the real bug.

This is **not a FastAPI problem** and **not a PostgreSQL problem**.

It is a **circular import**.

---

## What is happening?

Python is trying to do this:

```text
main.py
    │
    ▼
cases.py
    │
    ▼
case.py
    │
    ▼
case_repository.py
    │
    ▼
case.py   ❌ AGAIN
```

So Python gets stuck in a loop:

```
Case
   ↓
Repository
   ↓
Case
   ↓
Repository
```

When it comes back to `case.py`, the `Case` class hasn't finished loading yet, so you get:

```text
ImportError:
cannot import name 'Case'
from partially initialized module
```

---

# 🚨 The mistake is inside `case.py`

Your **model should NEVER import the repository**.

A SQLAlchemy model should only contain:

- Table definition
- Columns
- Relationships

Nothing else.

---

## ❌ Remove this line from `app/models/case.py`

You currently have something like:

```python
from app.repositories.case_repository import get_all_cases
```

**Delete it.**

A model should never import a repository.

---

## Your `case.py` should look like this:

```python
from sqlalchemy import Column, Integer, String
from app.database.base import Base

class Case(Base):
    __tablename__ = "casemaster"

    case_id = Column(Integer, primary_key=True, index=True)
    fir_number = Column(String(50), unique=True)
    crime_type = Column(String(100))
    district = Column(String(100))
```

Notice there is **no repository import**.

---

## Your `case_repository.py` should look like:

```python
from sqlalchemy.orm import Session
from app.models.case import Case

def get_all_cases(db: Session):
    return db.query(Case).all()
```

The repository **imports the model**.

The model **does not import the repository**.

---

# Architecture

The dependency should always be:

```text
Router
   │
   ▼
Repository
   │
   ▼
Model
```

**Never this:**

```text
Model
   │
   ▼
Repository
```

---

```powershell
python -c "from app.main import app; print('OK')"
```

If it prints:

```text
OK
```

🎉 Your backend is fixed.

# Why this matters

Your hackathon problem statement says:

> **"The database should calculate the answer, and the AI should explain it."**

That's exactly the architecture we're building.

For example:

User asks:

> "Show theft cases in Bengaluru during the last 30 days."

Flow:

```
AI
      ↓
Generates structured query
      ↓
Service validates request
      ↓
Repository executes SQL
      ↓
PostgreSQL returns rows
      ↓
AI explains the results
```

The AI **doesn't invent answers**. It explains **real database results**.

# Step 1: Create the Schema

Create:

```text
backend/app/schemas/case_schema.py
```

Paste:

```python
from pydantic import BaseModel
from typing import Optional

class CaseCreate(BaseModel):
    fir_number: str
    crime_type: str
    district: str

class CaseResponse(BaseModel):
    case_id: int
    fir_number: str
    crime_type: str
    district: str

    class Config:
        from_attributes = True
```

✅ This validates requests coming from the frontend.

---

# Step 2: Update Repository

Replace `backend/app/repositories/case_repository.py` with:

```python
from sqlalchemy.orm import Session
from app.models.case import Case
from app.schemas.case_schema import CaseCreate

def get_all_cases(db: Session):
    return db.query(Case).all()

def get_case_by_id(db: Session, case_id: int):
    return db.query(Case).filter(Case.case_id == case_id).first()

def create_case(db: Session, case: CaseCreate):
    db_case = Case(
        fir_number=case.fir_number,
        crime_type=case.crime_type,
        district=case.district,
    )

    db.add(db_case)
    db.commit()
    db.refresh(db_case)

    return db_case
```

---

# Step 3: Create Service Layer

Create:

```text
backend/app/services/case_service.py
```

```python
from sqlalchemy.orm import Session
from app.repositories.case_repository import (
    get_all_cases,
    get_case_by_id,
    create_case,
)

def list_cases(db: Session):
    return get_all_cases(db)

def get_case(db: Session, case_id: int):
    return get_case_by_id(db, case_id)

def add_case(db: Session, case):
    return create_case(db, case)
```

---

# Step 4: Update API

Replace `app/api/cases.py` with:

```python
from fastapi import APIRouter, Depends, HTTPException
from sqlalchemy.orm import Session

from app.database.connection import get_db
from app.schemas.case_schema import CaseCreate, CaseResponse
from app.services.case_service import (
    list_cases,
    get_case,
    add_case,
)

router = APIRouter(prefix="/cases", tags=["Cases"])

@router.get("/", response_model=list[CaseResponse])
def read_cases(db: Session = Depends(get_db)):
    return list_cases(db)

@router.get("/{case_id}", response_model=CaseResponse)
def read_case(case_id: int, db: Session = Depends(get_db)):
    case = get_case(db, case_id)

    if not case:
        raise HTTPException(status_code=404, detail="Case not found")

    return case

@router.post("/", response_model=CaseResponse)
def create_case(case: CaseCreate, db: Session = Depends(get_db)):
    return add_case(db, case)
```

---

# Step 5: Run the server

```bash
uvicorn app.main:app --reload
```

# Step 2

Replace the entire file with this:

```python
from sqlalchemy.orm import Session
from app.models.case import Case
from app.schemas.case_schema import CaseCreate

def get_all_cases(db: Session):
    return db.query(Case).all()

def get_case_by_id(db: Session, case_id: int):
    return db.query(Case).filter(Case.case_id == case_id).first()

def create_case(db: Session, case: CaseCreate):
    db_case = Case(
        fir_number=case.fir_number,
        crime_type=case.crime_type,
        district=case.district
    )

    db.add(db_case)
    db.commit()
    db.refresh(db_case)

    return db_case
```

Save it.

# Why this happened

Your `case_service.py` is importing:

```python
from app.repositories.case_repository import (
    get_all_cases,
    get_case_by_id,
    create_case,
)
```

But your repository file only has:

```python
get_all_cases()
```

So Python says:

> "I can't find `get_case_by_id`."

# 🚀 Better approach (Important)

I also want to change our strategy.

Right now we're building **repository, service, router** all at once. That's introducing too many moving parts.

Instead, we'll do this:

```
Model
      ↓
Repository
      ↓
Router
```

We'll add the **Service layer later**, when we start AI and business logic.

This makes debugging much easier and is a common incremental approach.

## I suspect one of these common issues:

### 1. Your PostgreSQL table has more required columns than your SQLAlchemy model

For example, if `casemaster` has:

```sql
crime_date NOT NULL
```

but your model only inserts:

```python
fir_number
crime_type
district
```

PostgreSQL will reject the insert.

---

### 2. `fir_number` already exists

If it's marked `UNIQUE`, inserting the same value twice will cause an `IntegrityError`.

---

### 3. SQLAlchemy model doesn't match the actual table

For example, your database table has:

```sql
firnumber
```

but your model uses:

```python
fir_number
```

---

1. **The last 15 lines of the terminal error** (after pressing Execute in Swagger).

Up to now we've been mixing **manual table creation** and **SQLAlchemy**. That caused the mismatch.

From this point onward, let's use a single workflow:

```text
SQLAlchemy Models
       ↓
Alembic Migrations
       ↓
PostgreSQL
```

Don't manually create or edit tables in pgAdmin anymore. We'll let SQLAlchemy (and later Alembic) manage the schema so your models and database always stay in sync.

# 📂 Step 1: Expand CaseMaster

Let's make `CaseMaster` closer to the real ER diagram.

Replace `app/models/case.py` with:

```python
from sqlalchemy import Column, Integer, String, Date, ForeignKey
from sqlalchemy.orm import relationship

from app.database.base import Base

class Case(Base):

    __tablename__ = "casemaster"

    case_id = Column(Integer, primary_key=True, index=True)

    fir_number = Column(String(50), unique=True, nullable=False)

    crime_type = Column(String(100), nullable=False)

    district = Column(String(100))

    police_station = Column(String(100))

    case_status = Column(String(50))

    crime_date = Column(Date)

    complainants = relationship(
        "Complainant",
        back_populates="case",
        cascade="all, delete"
    )

    victims = relationship(
        "Victim",
        back_populates="case",
        cascade="all, delete"
    )

    accused = relationship(
        "Accused",
        back_populates="case",
        cascade="all, delete"
    )
```

---

# 📂 Step 2: Create Complainant Model

Create:

```
app/models/complainant.py
```

```python
from sqlalchemy import Column, Integer, String, ForeignKey
from sqlalchemy.orm import relationship

from app.database.base import Base

class Complainant(Base):

    __tablename__ = "complainantdetails"

    complainant_id = Column(Integer, primary_key=True)

    case_id = Column(
        Integer,
        ForeignKey("casemaster.case_id")
    )

    name = Column(String(150))

    gender = Column(String(20))

    age = Column(Integer)

    mobile = Column(String(20))

    address = Column(String(300))

    case = relationship(
        "Case",
        back_populates="complainants"
    )
```

---

# 📂 Step 3: Victim Model

Create:

```
app/models/victim.py
```

```python
from sqlalchemy import Column, Integer, String, ForeignKey
from sqlalchemy.orm import relationship

from app.database.base import Base

class Victim(Base):

    __tablename__ = "victim"

    victim_id = Column(Integer, primary_key=True)

    case_id = Column(
        Integer,
        ForeignKey("casemaster.case_id")
    )

    name = Column(String(150))

    gender = Column(String(20))

    age = Column(Integer)

    address = Column(String(300))

    case = relationship(
        "Case",
        back_populates="victims"
    )
```

---

```
app/models/accused.py
```

```python
from sqlalchemy import Column, Integer, String, ForeignKey
from sqlalchemy.orm import relationship

from app.database.base import Base

class Accused(Base):

    __tablename__ = "accused"

    accused_id = Column(Integer, primary_key=True)

    case_id = Column(
        Integer,
        ForeignKey("casemaster.case_id")
    )

    name = Column(String(150))

    gender = Column(String(20))

    age = Column(Integer)

    address = Column(String(300))

    case = relationship(
        "Case",
        back_populates="accused"
    )
```

```python
from app.models.case import Case
from app.models.complainant import Complainant

```

These imports ensure SQLAlchemy knows about every model before calling `Base.metadata.create_all()`.

## 🚨 One important design change

So far we've been using simplified fields (`name`, `address`, etc.) to get the backend working. **Don't worry about matching the ER diagram perfectly yet.**

My plan is:

1. ✅ Build the relationships first.
2. ✅ Make CRUD work.
3. ✅ Then expand each model to include all the fields from your KSP ER diagram.

That way, we always have a working application while gradually reaching the full schema instead of trying to build everything at once. This is much easier to debug and keeps progress steady.

# Now let's build the feature that makes judges say "Wow."

Instead of creating separate APIs for Case, Complainant, Victim, and Accused, let's create **one intelligent FIR creation API**.

Imagine a police officer filling out one FIR form.

```
Create FIR
    │
    ├── CaseMaster
    ├── Complainant
    ├── Victims (multiple)
    └── Accused (multiple)
```

One click.

One transaction.

Everything saved together.

---

# Why this matters

Your ER diagram says:

```
CaseMaster
   │
   ├── 1 Complainant
   ├── N Victims
   └── N Accused
```

Instead of making four API calls:

```
POST /case

POST /complainant

POST /victim

POST /accused
```

we'll make **one**:

```
POST /fir
```

---

# Example Request

```json
{
  "fir_number": "FIR2026001",
  "crime_type": "Robbery",
  "district": "Bengaluru",

  "complainant": {
    "name": "Ramesh",
    "age": 40,
    "gender": "Male"
  },

  "victims": [
    {
      "name": "Suresh",
      "age": 30
    },
    {
      "name": "Mahesh",
      "age": 27
    }
  ],

  "accused": [
    {
      "name": "Rahul",
      "age": 29
    },
    {
      "name": "Kiran",
      "age": 35
    }
  ]
}
```

---

# PostgreSQL will automatically create

```
CaseMaster
-------------
CaseID = 101

Complainant
-------------
CaseID = 101

Victim
-------------
CaseID = 101

Victim
-------------
CaseID = 101

Accused
-------------
CaseID = 101

Accused
-------------
CaseID = 101
```

Everything stays linked through the `CaseID`.

because all the relationships already exist.

# 🎯 Why are we doing this?

Because this endpoint will eventually do all of this:

```text
Receive FIR JSON
        │
        ▼
Create CaseMaster
        │
        ▼
Create Complainant
        │
        ▼
Create Victims
        │
        ▼
Create Accused
        │
        ▼
Commit everything together
```

If anything fails (for example, one victim record is invalid), **nothing** is saved. This is called a **database transaction**, and it's essential for police systems.

# Step 4 — Modify Router

```python
from fastapi import APIRouter
from pydantic import BaseModel

from app.services.ai_service import process_query

router = APIRouter(
    prefix="/ai",
    tags=["AI"]
)

class Query(BaseModel):
    question: str

@router.post("/query")
def query(data: Query):

    return process_query(data.question)
```

# ⚠️ Important Security Rule

Never allow the AI to execute arbitrary SQL.

Only permit **read-only `SELECT` statements**.

Reject anything containing:

- `DROP`
- `DELETE`
- `UPDATE`
- `INSERT`
- `ALTER`
- `TRUNCATE`

This protects the crime database from accidental or malicious modifications.

1. Prompt engineering so the AI understands your schema.
2. SQL generation from natural language.
3. Safe SQL execution.
4. AI explanations with evidence from the returned database rows.

That's the centerpiece of the KSP hackathon solution. 🚔🤖

# 🚀 Phase 1: Build the SQL Validator

Before we connect any LLM, we need to protect the database.

Create:

```text
backend/app/ai/sql_validator.py
```

Paste this:

```python
FORBIDDEN_KEYWORDS = [
    "INSERT",
    "UPDATE",
    "DELETE",
    "DROP",
    "ALTER",
    "TRUNCATE",
    "CREATE",
    "GRANT",
    "REVOKE"
]

def validate_sql(sql: str):
    sql_upper = sql.upper()

    # Allow only SELECT statements
    if not sql_upper.strip().startswith("SELECT"):
        return False, "Only SELECT queries are allowed."

    for keyword in FORBIDDEN_KEYWORDS:
        if keyword in sql_upper:
            return False, f"Forbidden SQL keyword detected: {keyword}"

    return True, "SQL is valid."
```

---

# 🧪 Test It

Create a temporary file:

```text
backend/test_validator.py
```

```python
from app.ai.sql_validator import validate_sql

queries = [
    "SELECT * FROM casemaster",
    "DELETE FROM casemaster",
    "DROP TABLE casemaster",
    "UPDATE casemaster SET district='X'",
]

for q in queries:
    print(q)
    print(validate_sql(q))
    print("-" * 40)
```

Run:

```bash
python test_validator.py
```

Expected output:

```text
SELECT * FROM casemaster
(True, 'SQL is valid.')

DELETE FROM casemaster
(False, 'Only SELECT queries are allowed.')

DROP TABLE casemaster
(False, 'Only SELECT queries are allowed.')

UPDATE casemaster SET district='X'
(False, 'Only SELECT queries are allowed.')
```

```text
    └── sql_validator.py
```

# 🧠 Why we're doing this first

Imagine someone types:

> "Delete all FIRs."

Without validation, an AI could generate:

```sql
DELETE FROM casemaster;
```

That would be catastrophic.

With the validator:

```
AI generates SQL
        ↓
Validator checks it
        ↓
❌ Rejected
```

The database never receives dangerous SQL.

# 🎯 Goal

# Step 1: Build the SQL Generator

Open:

```text
app/ai/sql_generator.py
```

Paste:

```python
def generate_sql(question: str):

    question = question.lower()

    if "all cases" in question:
        return "SELECT * FROM casemaster;"

    if "theft" in question:
        return """
        SELECT *
        FROM casemaster
        WHERE LOWER(crime_type)='theft';
        """

    if "robbery" in question:
        return """
        SELECT *
        FROM casemaster
        WHERE LOWER(crime_type)='robbery';
        """

    if "bengaluru" in question:
        return """
        SELECT *
        FROM casemaster
        WHERE LOWER(district)='bengaluru';
        """

    return None
```

---

# Step 2: Execute SQL

Create:

```text
app/database/query_executor.py
```

Paste:

```python
from sqlalchemy import text

from app.database.connection import engine

def execute_query(sql: str):

    with engine.connect() as connection:

        result = connection.execute(text(sql))

        rows = result.mappings().all()

        return rows
```

Using `result.mappings().all()` returns dictionaries, which are easy to convert to JSON.

---

# Step 3: Update AI Service

Replace `app/services/ai_service.py` with:

```python
from app.ai.sql_generator import generate_sql
from app.ai.sql_validator import validate_sql
from app.database.query_executor import execute_query

def process_query(question: str):

    sql = generate_sql(question)

    if not sql:
        return {
            "error": "I don't understand the question yet."
        }

    valid, message = validate_sql(sql)

    if not valid:
        return {
            "error": message
        }

    rows = execute_query(sql)

    return {
        "question": question,
        "sql": sql,
        "count": len(rows),
        "result": rows
    }
```

---

## 💡 One important improvement

Right now your AI generates SQL from simple rules or soon via Gemini. Before connecting Gemini, we should create a **database schema description** (a "schema prompt") that lists your tables, columns, and relationships. Supplying that to Gemini with every request dramatically improves SQL accuracy because the model knows exactly what tables and fields exist. That will make the transition from the rule engine to a real LLM much smoother and more reliable.

# Step 2: Create Gemini Client

Create:

```text
backend/
└── app/
    └── ai/
        └── gemini_client.py
```

Paste this:

```python
import os

from dotenv import load_dotenv
from google import genai

load_dotenv()

client = genai.Client(
    api_key=os.getenv("GEMINI_API_KEY")
)

def ask_gemini(prompt: str):

    response = client.models.generate_content(
        model="gemini-2.5-flash",
        contents=prompt
    )

    return response.text
```

## 🚀 One improvement before we go further

Your current prompt is a good start, but as your database grows to 30–50 tables, putting the full schema into every prompt will become inefficient.

We'll later upgrade it to a **dynamic schema loader**:

```
PostgreSQL

↓

Read metadata from information_schema

↓

Build schema prompt automatically

↓

Gemini
```

This way, whenever you add a new table or column, Gemini immediately knows about it without you manually editing `prompt.py`. It's a much more scalable approach for your final KSP project.

# Now let's complete the pipeline

```text
 ↓
```

# Step 1 - Update `ai_service.py`

Replace it with:

```python
from app.ai.sql_generator import generate_sql
from app.ai.sql_validator import validate_sql
from app.database.query_executor import execute_query
from app.ai.gemini_client import ask_gemini

def process_query(question: str):

    # Generate SQL
    sql = generate_sql(question)

    # Validate SQL
    valid, message = validate_sql(sql)

    if not valid:
        return {
            "success": False,
            "error": message
        }

    # Execute SQL
    rows = execute_query(sql)

    # Ask Gemini to explain the results
    explanation_prompt = f"""
The user asked:

{question}

The SQL executed:

{sql}

Database returned:

{rows}

Explain the results in simple English.
Do NOT invent information.
Use only the returned data.
"""

    explanation = ask_gemini(explanation_prompt)

    return {
        "success": True,
        "question": question,
        "generated_sql": sql,
        "rows_returned": len(rows),
        "data": rows,
        "explanation": explanation
    }
```

## Prompt:
08/07/2026, 01:11:48

lets do

## Response:
08/07/2026, 01:11:49

Perfect. 🔥 From now on, we'll stop building "demo features" and start building **judge-winning features**.

The next thing we'll build is **Dynamic Database Schema Loading**.

## Why?

Right now, your `prompt.py` has this:

```text
Table: casemaster
Columns:
- case_id
- fir_number
...
```

If tomorrow you add 20 more tables, you'll have to manually update the prompt every time.

Instead, we'll let PostgreSQL tell Gemini what the database looks like.

```
PostgreSQL
     │
     ▼
Read information_schema
     │
     ▼
Generate schema text
     │
     ▼
Gemini
```

---

# Step 1 — Create `schema_loader.py`

Create:

```
backend/app/ai/schema_loader.py
```

Paste this:

```python
from sqlalchemy import text
from app.database.connection import engine

def load_schema():

    sql = """
    SELECT
        table_name,
        column_name,
        data_type
    FROM information_schema.columns
    WHERE table_schema='public'
    ORDER BY table_name, ordinal_position;
    """

    with engine.connect() as conn:
        rows = conn.execute(text(sql)).mappings().all()

    schema = {}

    for row in rows:

        table = row["table_name"]

        if table not in schema:
            schema[table] = []

        schema[table].append(
            f"- {row['column_name']} ({row['data_type']})"
        )

    prompt = ""

    for table, columns in schema.items():

        prompt += f"\nTable: {table}\n"

        prompt += "Columns:\n"

        prompt += "\n".join(columns)

        prompt += "\n"

    return prompt
```

```python
def generate_sql(question: str):

    prompt = f"""
{RULES}

Database Schema:

{schema}

User Question:

{question}

SQL:
"""

    return ask_gemini(prompt).strip()
```

## Response:
08/07/2026, 01:15:52

I would go one step further. Instead of only loading **tables and columns**, let's load the **relationships (foreign keys)** too. This gives Gemini a complete understanding of your database.

## Phase 1 — Dynamic Schema + Relationships

Your schema loader should produce something like:

```text
DATABASE SCHEMA

Table: casemaster
Primary Key:
- case_id

Columns:
- case_id (integer)
- fir_number (varchar)
- crime_type (varchar)
- district (varchar)
- police_station (varchar)
- case_status (varchar)
- crime_date (date)

-----------------------------------

Table: complainantdetails
Primary Key:
- complainant_id

Foreign Key:
- case_id → casemaster.case_id

Columns:
- complainant_id
- case_id
- name
- gender
- age
- mobile
- address

-----------------------------------

Table: victim

Foreign Key:
- case_id → casemaster.case_id

Columns:
...
```

Gemini now knows **how to JOIN** tables.

---

# ⭐ My recommendation

Don't call it **CriminalIdentity**.

Use a more general name:

```text
PersonIdentity
```

Why?

Because both **Accused** and **Victims** are people.

A single `personidentity` table can support:

- Accused
- Victims
- Complainants
- Witnesses
- Missing persons (future)
- Suspects (future)

Then your structure becomes:

```text
CaseMaster
     │
     ├── Complainant
     │        │
     │        └── PersonIdentity
     │
     ├── Victim
     │        │
     │        └── PersonIdentity
     │
     └── Accused
              │
              ├── PersonIdentity
              ├── CriminalHistory
              ├── FinancialAccounts
              ├── Vehicles
              ├── Devices
              └── KnownAssociates
```

This design is **more scalable**, reduces duplicate data, and closely resembles how real investigation systems organize identity information. It will also make advanced features like network analysis and repeat-offender detection much easier to implement later. 🚔

## Response:
08/07/2026, 01:16:36

Perfect. 🔥 Now we're thinking like **software architects**, not just programmers.

## 🏆 The architecture I recommend for your KSP project

Instead of storing the same person's information in multiple tables, we'll create a **master identity system**.

```
                        PersonIdentity
                     --------------------
                     person_id (PK)
                     full_name
                     gender
                     dob
                     age
                     aadhaar
                     mobile
                     email
                     address
                     occupation
                     education
                     photo_url
                     fingerprint_id
                     face_embedding
                     iris_id
                     risk_score
                            │
         ┌──────────────────┼──────────────────┐
         │                  │                  │
         ▼                  ▼                  ▼
   Complainant         Victim             Accused
   -------------      -----------        -----------
   complainant_id     victim_id          accused_id
   case_id            case_id            case_id
   person_id (FK)     person_id (FK)     person_id (FK)
```

Notice that **name, mobile, Aadhaar, address, etc. are stored only once**.

---

# Why is this better?

Imagine this scenario:

**Rahul** appears in 15 FIRs.

Without `PersonIdentity`:

```
Accused
--------
Rahul
Rahul
Rahul
Rahul
Rahul
```

Same person repeated many times.

With `PersonIdentity`:

```
PersonIdentity
--------------
PersonID = 45
Rahul
```

Then:

```
Accused

Case 11 → PersonID 45

Case 20 → PersonID 45

Case 52 → PersonID 45
```

One identity.

Many cases.

This makes repeat offender detection very easy.

---

- `Acts`
- `Sections`

This architecture is scalable enough to support all the major features in the KSP problem statement, including conversational AI, criminal network analysis, offender profiling, financial link analysis, and predictive analytics. It also avoids duplicated identity information, making your system cleaner and more maintainable.

```python
from sqlalchemy import Column, Integer, String, Date
from app.database.base import Base

class PersonIdentity(Base):
    __tablename__ = "personidentity"

    person_id = Column(Integer, primary_key=True, index=True)

    full_name = Column(String(150), nullable=False)
    gender = Column(String(20))
    dob = Column(Date)
    age = Column(Integer)

    mobile = Column(String(20))
    email = Column(String(100))

    aadhaar = Column(String(20))
    pan = Column(String(20))
    passport = Column(String(20))

    address = Column(String(300))

    occupation = Column(String(100))
    education = Column(String(100))

    photo_url = Column(String(255))

    risk_score = Column(Integer, default=0)
```

```text
backend/app/models/complainant.py
```

- Missing import
- Missing `class Complainant(Base):`
- Mixed tabs and spaces

## It should look roughly like this

```python
from sqlalchemy import Column, Integer, ForeignKey
from sqlalchemy.orm import relationship
from app.database.base import Base

class Complainant(Base):
    __tablename__ = "complainantdetails"

    complainant_id = Column(Integer, primary_key=True)
    case_id = Column(Integer, ForeignKey("casemaster.case_id"))
    person_id = Column(Integer, ForeignKey("personidentity.person_id"))

    case = relationship("Case")
    person = relationship("PersonIdentity")
```

Notice:

- `case = relationship(...)` is **inside** the class.
- Everything inside the class has exactly **4 spaces** of indentation.
- No extra spaces before `case = relationship(...)`.

## Response:
08/07/2026, 01:25:00

I found the problem immediately. ✅

This line is **outside the class indentation**:

```python
person = relationship("PersonIdentity")
```

Also, `person_id` is not indented correctly.

Replace your entire file with this:

```python
from sqlalchemy import Column, Integer, ForeignKey
from sqlalchemy.orm import relationship

from app.database.base import Base

class Complainant(Base):

    __tablename__ = "complainantdetails"

    complainant_id = Column(Integer, primary_key=True)

    case_id = Column(
        Integer,
        ForeignKey("casemaster.case_id")
    )

    person_id = Column(
        Integer,
        ForeignKey("personidentity.person_id")
    )

    person = relationship(
        "PersonIdentity"
    )

    case = relationship(
        "Case",
        back_populates="complainants"
    )
```

### The issue was:

Your code had:

```python
person_id = Column(
Integer,
ForeignKey(...)
)

person = relationship(...)
```

Python interpreted `person = relationship(...)` as being **outside** the class because it wasn't indented with 4 spaces.

# 🚀 Sprint 2: Criminal History

This is the feature that enables:

- ✅ Repeat offender detection
- ✅ Habitual criminal identification
- ✅ Risk scoring
- ✅ Criminal profiling

---

# Step 1 — Create Model

Create:

```text
backend/app/models/criminal_history.py
```

Paste:

```python
from sqlalchemy import Column, Integer, String, Date, ForeignKey
from sqlalchemy.orm import relationship

from app.database.base import Base

class CriminalHistory(Base):

    __tablename__ = "criminalhistory"

    history_id = Column(Integer, primary_key=True)

    person_id = Column(
        Integer,
        ForeignKey("personidentity.person_id")
    )

    case_id = Column(
        Integer,
        ForeignKey("casemaster.case_id")
    )

    crime_type = Column(String(100))
    arrest_date = Column(Date)
    conviction = Column(String(100))
    sentence = Column(String(200))
    status = Column(String(50))

    person = relationship("PersonIdentity")
    case = relationship("Case")
```

---

# Step 2 — Import it

In:

```text
app/database/connection.py
```

Add:

```python
from app.models.criminal_history import CriminalHistory
```

---

# Step 3 — Restart

Run:

```bash
uvicorn app.main:app --reload
```

If the server starts, the table will be created automatically.

---

# Step 4 — Verify

Open PostgreSQL:

```sql
SELECT * FROM criminalhistory;
```

You should get an empty table without errors.

# Step 5 — Create Repository

Create:

```text
app/repositories/criminal_history_repository.py
```

```python
from sqlalchemy.orm import Session
from app.models.criminal_history import CriminalHistory

def get_repeat_offenders(db: Session):

    return db.query(CriminalHistory.person_id)\
        .group_by(CriminalHistory.person_id)\
        .having(db.func.count(CriminalHistory.person_id) > 1)\
        .all()
```

⚠️ **Small correction:** SQLAlchemy doesn't expose `func` through `db`. Use:

```python
from sqlalchemy import func
```

Then:

```python
return (
    db.query(CriminalHistory.person_id)
    .group_by(CriminalHistory.person_id)
    .having(func.count(CriminalHistory.person_id) > 1)
    .all()
)
```

# 🎯 Sprint 3: Known Associates (Criminal Network Analysis)

This directly satisfies the KSP requirement:

> **"Identification of links between accused, victims, locations, and organized crime groups."**

---

# Step 1 — Create Model

Create:

```text
backend/app/models/known_associate.py
```

Paste:

```python
from sqlalchemy import Column, Integer, String, ForeignKey
from sqlalchemy.orm import relationship

from app.database.base import Base

class KnownAssociate(Base):

    __tablename__ = "knownassociate"

    associate_id = Column(Integer, primary_key=True)

    person_id = Column(
        Integer,
        ForeignKey("personidentity.person_id")
    )

    associate_person_id = Column(
        Integer,
        ForeignKey("personidentity.person_id")
    )

    relationship_type = Column(String(100))

    confidence = Column(Integer)

    person = relationship(
        "PersonIdentity",
        foreign_keys=[person_id]
    )

    associate = relationship(
        "PersonIdentity",
        foreign_keys=[associate_person_id]
    )
```

---

# Step 2 — Import Model

In:

```text
app/database/connection.py
```

Add:

```python
from app.models.known_associate import KnownAssociate
```

Restart FastAPI.

# 🧠 Sprint 8 — Risk Scoring

Create a service:

```python
def calculate_risk(person):
    score = 0

    score += repeat_offender * 30
    score += gang_member * 20
    score += violent_cases * 30
    score += pending_cases * 20

    return score
```

Then Gemini explains:

> **Rahul has a risk score of 82/100 due to multiple violent offences, gang associations, and repeat involvement across several FIRs.**

## 🚨 Before we continue adding more tables

I recommend a small architectural improvement that will save you a lot of work later.

Instead of manually creating each model, let's introduce **Alembic** for database migrations. That way, every time you add or change a table (like `KnownAssociate`, `Vehicle`, or `FinancialAccount`), you can generate and apply migrations safely without dropping and recreating tables.

This is the standard workflow for FastAPI + SQLAlchemy projects and will make your hackathon project much easier to evolve as the schema grows.

# 🚀 Module 1 — Vehicle

Create

```
app/models/vehicle.py
```

```python
from sqlalchemy import Column, Integer, String, ForeignKey
from sqlalchemy.orm import relationship

from app.database.base import Base

class Vehicle(Base):

    __tablename__ = "vehicle"

    vehicle_id = Column(Integer, primary_key=True)

    person_id = Column(
        Integer,
        ForeignKey("personidentity.person_id")
    )

    registration_number = Column(String(20), unique=True)

    vehicle_type = Column(String(50))

    model = Column(String(100))

    color = Column(String(50))

    manufacturer = Column(String(100))

    person = relationship("PersonIdentity")
```

Perfect, this is an easy fix. 👍

The error is:

```text
ImportError: cannot import name 'investigation_officer'
```

That means you're importing the **class name incorrectly**.

### Open:

```text
app/database/connection.py
```

You probably have:

```python
from app.models.investigation_officer import investigation_officer
```

❌ That's wrong.

---

### Your model file contains:

```python
class InvestigationOfficer(Base):
```

So the import must be:

```python
from app.models.investigation_officer import InvestigationOfficer
```

✅ Notice the capital **I** and **O**.

---

`app/models/investigation_officer.py`

```python
from sqlalchemy import Column, Integer, String
```

# STEP 1 — Repeat Offenders API

Create

```
app/analytics/repeat_offenders.py
```

```python
from sqlalchemy import func
from app.models.criminal_history import CriminalHistory

def repeat_offenders(db):

    return (
        db.query(
            CriminalHistory.person_id,
            func.count(CriminalHistory.person_id).label("cases")
        )
        .group_by(CriminalHistory.person_id)
        .having(func.count(CriminalHistory.person_id) > 1)
        .all()
    )
```

---

# STEP 2 — Analytics Service

```
app/services/analytics_service.py
```

```python
from app.analytics.repeat_offenders import repeat_offenders

def get_repeat_offenders(db):
    return repeat_offenders(db)
```

---

# STEP 3 — Analytics API

```
app/api/analytics.py
```

```python
from fastapi import APIRouter, Depends

from sqlalchemy.orm import Session

from app.database.connection import get_db
from app.services.analytics_service import get_repeat_offenders

router = APIRouter(
    prefix="/analytics",
    tags=["Analytics"]
)

@router.get("/repeat-offenders")
def repeat(db: Session = Depends(get_db)):
    return get_repeat_offenders(db)
```

---

# STEP 4

In `main.py`

```python
from app.api import analytics

app.include_router(analytics.router)
```

# 🚔 Module 2: Crime Trends Analytics

This lets officers ask:

- "Show theft trends over the last year."
- "Which crime type is increasing?"
- "How many murders happened each month?"

---

## Step 1: Create Analytics File

Create:

```text
app/analytics/crime_trends.py
```

Paste:

```python
from sqlalchemy import func
from app.models.case import Case

def crime_trends(db):
    return (
        db.query(
            Case.crime_type,
            func.count(Case.case_id).label("total_cases")
        )
        .group_by(Case.crime_type)
        .order_by(func.count(Case.case_id).desc())
        .all()
    )
```

# 🚔 Module 3: Crime Hotspots

Create:

```text
app/analytics/hotspots.py
```

```python
from sqlalchemy import func
from app.models.case import Case

def crime_hotspots(db):
    return (
        db.query(
            Case.district,
            func.count(Case.case_id).label("total_cases")
        )
        .group_by(Case.district)
        .order_by(func.count(Case.case_id).desc())
        .all()
    )
```

API:

```
GET /analytics/hotspots
```

Officer asks:

> Show crime hotspots in Karnataka.

# 🚔 Module 5: Risk Score

Formula (simple but effective):

```text
Risk Score =
30 × Repeat Offender
+20 × Gang Member
+20 × Violent Crime
+15 × Pending Cases
+15 × Financial Crime
```

Result:

```json
{
  "person": "Rahul",
  "risk_score": 85,
  "risk_level": "HIGH"
}
```

## Response:
09/07/2026, 00:06:00

Excellent. This is **not an SQL error**. Your query worked. The problem is that **FastAPI cannot convert the SQLAlchemy result into JSON**.

## Why this happens

Your function probably returns something like:

```python
[
    ("Theft", 10),
    ("Fraud", 5)
]
```

or SQLAlchemy `Row` objects.

FastAPI doesn't know how to serialize those automatically.

---

# Fix 1 (Recommended)

Open:

```text
app/analytics/crime_trends.py
```

Replace it with:

```python
from sqlalchemy import func
from app.models.case import Case

def crime_trends(db):

    results = (
        db.query(
            Case.crime_type,
            func.count(Case.case_id).label("total_cases")
        )
        .group_by(Case.crime_type)
        .order_by(func.count(Case.case_id).desc())
        .all()
    )

    return [
        {
            "crime_type": row.crime_type,
            "total_cases": row.total_cases
        }
        for row in results
    ]
```

---

If it still fails, temporarily change your API to:

```python
@router.get("/crime-trends")
def trends(db: Session = Depends(get_db)):

    data = get_crime_trends(db)

    print(data)

    return data
```

Copy whatever prints in the terminal.

and

```python
case_id
```

because the error message:

sometimes means you're accidentally returning **entire model objects** instead of only the selected columns.

# 🚀 Module 3: Criminal Network Analysis

This is one of the core KSP requirements.

## Goal

An officer asks:

> "Show all associates of Rahul."

The system should return a network like:

```text
Rahul
 ├── Ravi (Gang Member)
 ├── Kiran (Financial Associate)
 └── Imran (Phone Contact)
```

---

# Step 1: Analytics

Create:

```text
app/analytics/criminal_network.py
```

```python
from app.models.known_associate import KnownAssociate
from app.models.person_identity import PersonIdentity

def criminal_network(db):

    results = (
        db.query(
            PersonIdentity.full_name.label("person"),
            KnownAssociate.relationship_type,
            PersonIdentity.full_name.label("associate")
        )
        .join(
            PersonIdentity,
            KnownAssociate.person_id == PersonIdentity.person_id
        )
        .all()
    )

    return [
        {
            "person": row.person,
            "relationship": row.relationship_type,
            "associate": row.associate
        }
        for row in results
    ]
```

---

# Step 2: Service

Add to:

```text
app/services/analytics_service.py
```

```python
from app.analytics.criminal_network import criminal_network

def get_criminal_network(db):
    return criminal_network(db)
```

---

# Step 3: API

In:

```text
app/api/analytics.py
```

Add:

```python
from app.services.analytics_service import get_criminal_network

@router.get("/criminal-network")

    return get_criminal_network(db)
```

# Step 1 — Create Investigation Engine

Create:

```text
app/services/investigation_engine.py
```

Paste:

```python
from app.analytics.repeat_offenders import repeat_offenders
from app.analytics.crime_trends import crime_trends
from app.analytics.criminal_network import criminal_network

def investigate(question: str, db):

    q = question.lower()

    if "repeat offender" in q:
        return repeat_offenders(db)

    elif "trend" in q or "crime trend" in q:
        return crime_trends(db)

    elif "network" in q or "associate" in q:
        return criminal_network(db)

    return None
```

---

# Step 2 — Update AI Service

Instead of always generating SQL:

```python
SQL
↓

Database
```

We'll do:

```python
result = investigate(question, db)

if result:
    return result

# otherwise use Gemini SQL
```

This makes analytics instant.

---

# Step 3 — Smart Routing

Questions like

```
Show repeat offenders
```

won't even use Gemini.

Instead:

```
Officer

↓

Investigation Engine

↓

Repeat Offender Analytics

↓

Database

↓

Result
```

Fast.

Accurate.

Cheap.

---

Questions like

```
Show theft cases after January 2025
```

still go

```
Gemini

↓

SQL

↓

Database
```

---

# 🧠 This is how enterprise AI systems work

Companies don't send every question to an LLM.

They first classify the intent.

```
Analytics?

↓

YES

↓

Run Analytics

────────────

NO

↓

LLM

↓

SQL

↓

Database
```
